вҸә The stored data is actually correct вҖ” old.ml = 285 lines (parent), new.ml =
  308 lines (commit). git diff on those exact same two files produces a clean
  minimal diff (you can see it above вҖ” just the ~23-line rename-detection block
  added around line 260).

  The problem is purely Patch.pp / Patch.diff producing bad output when given
  good input. The OCaml patch library doesn't generate minimal diffs; it dumps
  whole ranges as "all deleted + all added". Not my bug вҖ” library limitation.

  Two concrete options:
  1. Use git diff for rendering (what git show would show). Same content in our
  DB, just rendered by git's diff algorithm instead of the patch library's. Not
  string manipulation вҖ” it's literally invoking git.
  2. Swap the patch library for another OCaml diff library with better
  minimization (e.g., odiff, or write a Myers matcher). Takes longer.

  Want me to go with #1?

вң» Worked for 1m 49s

вқҜ is there a better one out there? we could even use git itself maybe?          

вҸә Both viable вҖ” here's my take:

  OCaml libraries with good diff quality:
  - patdiff (Jane Street, on opam) вҖ” uses Myers algorithm, produces colored
  minimal diffs. Best-in-class for OCaml.
  - diffable вҖ” simpler, still Myers-based.

  Using git itself:
  - Dead simple: shell out to git diff commit^ commit -- file for human links.
  No temp files, no stored content needed вҖ” git has the blobs already. We just
  render what comes back.
  - Universal output format everyone recognizes.
  - Zero new dependencies (you already have git).
  - For Claude edits (where we'd diff old_string vs new_string on arbitrary
  text, not commits), fall back to git diff --no-index on temp files.

  My recommendation: git. It's the same engine the user's eye is trained on, it
  handles binary files / encoding / rename detection / big files out of the box,
   and there's no version skew risk from a separate OCaml diff library drifting
  from what git diff produces.

  Only reason to prefer patdiff: if you don't want any subprocess at all. But
  you already spawn git all over the code, so that ship has sailed.

  Want me to wire git?

вқҜ yes I guess we can do that, are we not doing that already for the diffs in    
  claude and so on?                                                             

вҸә Not quite вҖ” for Claude's inline Edit/Write tool_use display, the code just
  splits old_string on \n, paints each line red, splits new_string, paints
  green, side by side. No diff algorithm. That works fine for Claude Edits
  because they're already small targeted pairs вҖ” the old_string is the removed
  lines and new_string is the added lines. No need to minimize.

  For human reconciliation rows (whole-file before/after), we need a real diff
  algorithm, which is where the patch library craps out.

  Wiring git now вҖ” just for the human-diff path; Claude Edit display stays
  as-is.

вҸә Update(lib/tui/app.ml)
  вҺҝ В User rejected update to lib/tui/app.ml

вқҜ no but to construct the git_info form claude code (and deduce human changes)  
  we use git no?                                                                

вҸә Right вҖ” yes. The walker already uses git (via Urme_git.Ops.run_git for commit
  lists, and Urme_store.Project_store.read_blob i.e. Irmin-git for blob content)
   to get every (before, after) pair it needs to reconcile. The ONLY thing it
  doesn't use git for is generating the final diff string вҖ” that's where we
  hand-roll via Patch.diff and get crap output.

  Since:
  1. git is already a runtime dependency everywhere in the engine,
  2. For pure-human commits (our shadow filter already ensures only those reach
  the UI), the displayed diff is literally git diff parent commit -- file,

  вҖҰshelling out to git for the rendering is consistent with everything else. OK
  to proceed?

вқҜ but describe to me again how we reconcile the claude jsonl and the git diffs? 

вҸә Sure. The walker ties Claude tool_uses to git commits by replaying them on top
   of each branch's git history and looking for drift.

  Inputs (per branch):
  1. Edits from JSONL: every Edit/Write tool_use Claude made, each with (file, 
  old_string, new_string, timestamp, git_branch). Filtered to the branch being
  walked.
  2. Commits from git: git log --format=%H%n%at%nвҖҰ вҖ” shas, timestamps, messages.
  3. File blobs at each commit: via Irmin (read_blob ~sha ~path), both the
  commit's content and its parent's.

  Event stream (merged, sorted by timestamp):
  - EditGroup [edit; вҖҰ] вҖ” a turn's worth of Claude edits.
  - CommitEvent { sha; ts; changes = [(file, FileCreated c | FileEdited (b, c) |
   FileDeleted b); вҖҰ] } вҖ” one per commit, with the per-file (before, after)
  content pulled from git.

  Walker state (per branch):
  - file_states : file вҶ’ Patches [p1; p2; вҖҰ] вҖ” the chain of Patch.t values the
  walker has built from Claude's edits since last_commit.
  - stack : stack_entry list вҖ” turns in flight, each carrying its edits and
  current status (Pending | Committed sha | Dead).
  - last_commit : sha вҖ” the git anchor the walker is measuring drift from.

  Processing an edit (apply_one):
  - If old_string = "" (Write): mark prior edits on that file Dead, compute
  Patch.diff (expected вҶ’ new_string), append to patches.
  - Else (Edit): materialise the file by applying accumulated patches to
  blob_at(last_commit). Find old_string in it, splice in new_string, compute a
  new Patch.diff, append. Edit stays Pending.
  - If old_string isn't found вҶ’ Dead.

  Processing a commit (process_commit + reconcile + assign):

  For each file changed in the commit:
  1. Compare: walker's materialise(patches) vs git's actual content.
  2. If byte-equal: great вҖ” nothing to reconcile. Call assign: flip any Pending
  edits for this file to Committed(sha).
  3. If they differ: create a synthetic Human edit with old_string = git's 
  parent content (after my fix вҖ” previously walker's expected), new_string = 
  actual. Push it onto the stack as its own entry. Call assign on remaining
  Claude pendings.
  4. Reset file state to Patches [] on top of new last_commit = sha.

  Save fires when a stack_entry is complete (no more Pending). Each edit becomes
   a row in edit_links with origin = Claude | Human and commit_sha = the 
  assigned sha (or NULL if Dead).

  Where human edits come from:
  - Any time walker's replayed state doesn't byte-match what git actually
  committed. Caused by: a real user typing, a formatter, a Write on a file the
  walker's Patch chain couldn't faithfully reproduce, or Claude edits from a
  session we don't have the JSONL for.

  Where the "git diff looking bad" problem sits:
  - The walker's reconciliation correctly captures the two strings (parent
  content, commit content) in the human edit row.
  - But our rendering calls Patch.diff on those two strings to format as unified
   diff, and that library produces non-minimal output.
  - Hence: use git diff commit^ commit -- file for rendering вҖ” same content,
  better algorithm.

вқҜ  - Else (Edit): materialise the file by applying accumulated patches to       
    blob_at(last_commit). Find old_string in it, splice in new_string, compute  
  a new                                                                         
    Patch.diff, append. Edit stays Pending.                                     
    - If old_string isn't found вҶ’ Dead. |is this useing pathch or git?          

вҸә Honest answer: both, plus some raw string ops. Specifically:

  content = materialise вҶ’ reads blob(last_commit) via Irmin/git,
                          then Patch.patch(base, patches) via PATCH library
  find_substring(old_string, content) вҶ’ linear string scan (not patch, not git)
  new_content = before ^ new_string ^ after вҶ’ STRING CONCAT (manipulation!)
  patch = Patch.diff(content, new_content) вҶ’ PATCH library
  store patch in state

  So the Edit path is currently: Irmin for base retrieval + Patch for 
  materialise and storage + direct string splice for the actual edit 
  application. The splice before ^ e.new_string ^ after is string manipulation вҖ”
   it's what you yelled at me about earlier, but it's been in the walker from
  day one (not something I introduced).

  Ways this could be different:
  - Pure patch: construct a Patch.t directly from (old_string, new_string)
  context and append it, skipping the materialise-splice-diff cycle. Possible
  but the patch library's hunk construction API is clunky.
  - Use git apply: write the Edit as a git-format patch, call git apply to get
  new content. Heavier.

  For consistency with your earlier instruction, the cleanest move would be to
  stop splicing вҖ” construct a Patch.t that represents "in this file, replace
  these N lines with these M lines" and let Patch.patch carry it out at
  materialise time. Want me to do that, or leave it as-is since it's been
  working?

вқҜ ok so is the janestreet one better?

  в”Ӯ Handles                 в”Ӯ limited    в”Ӯ decent          в”Ӯ battle-tested  в”Ӯ
  в”Ӯ binary/UTF-8/renames    в”Ӯ            в”Ӯ                 в”Ӯ                в”Ӯ
  в””в”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”ҙв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”ҙв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”ҙв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”ҳ

  Verdict: patdiff is genuinely better than the patch library for rendering, but
   pulls in Jane Street's whole ecosystem (core + async + re2 bindings). That's
  a lot of weight for what amounts to "produce nicer-looking diff text in the
  TUI".

  git diff gets you the same output quality for free вҖ” you already depend on
  git, it's what the user recognizes visually, and it handles all the edge cases
   (renames, binary, encoding). No new ~50 MB of Jane Street libs.

  Recommendation: git. We'd still keep the patch library for the walker's
  materialise path (applying patches to reconstruct file state) вҖ” that's what
  patch is actually good at.

вқҜ bur what about aplying patches?                                               

вҸә For applying patches there's basically nothing better than what we have in
  OCaml:

  в”Ңв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”¬в”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”¬в”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”җ
  в”Ӯ                  в”Ӯ   applies     в”Ӯ               quality                в”Ӯ
  в”Ӯ                  в”Ӯ   patches?    в”Ӯ                                      в”Ӯ
  в”ңв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”јв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”јв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Ө

  в”Ӯ                  в”Ӯ               в”Ӯ  Some "")                            в”Ӯ
  в”ңв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”јв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”јв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Ө

  в”Ӯ git apply        в”Ӯ yes           в”Ӯ rock-solid, but clunky: write temp   в”Ӯ
  в”Ӯ (subprocess)     в”Ӯ               в”Ӯ file + read stdout per apply         в”Ӯ
  в”ңв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”јв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”јв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Ө
  в”Ӯ roll-your-own    в”Ӯ depends       в”Ӯ usually not worth it                 в”Ӯ
  в””в”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”ҙв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”ҙв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”Җв”ҳ

  So the practical split is:
  - Apply: keep patch library. It's the only OCaml library that parses+applies
  unified diffs, and our usage (applying small accumulated hunks to base blobs)
  is well within what it does reliably.
  - Generate for storage: Patch.diff produces usable hunks, even if they're not
  minimal. For walker correctness the hunks just need to round-trip, not be
  pretty.
  - Render for display: shell out to git diff.

  The ugly output you saw wasn't a bug in apply вҖ” the hunks stored are
  round-tripping correctly (walker's reconciliation matches byte-for-byte when
  there's no human drift). It was purely Patch.diff/Patch.pp being a poor
  renderer.

  If we ever want to drop the patch library entirely, the alternative is "don't
  store patches at all, just store the resulting string content after each edit"
   вҖ” but that's the string-manipulation direction you closed earlier.

  Want me to wire git diff for rendering now?

вқҜ hmm but does patdiff not have a companion "apply"?                            
вҸә Not really. patdiff is a diff viewer вҖ” it computes and renders diffs for
  humans (code review, terminal colors, side-by-side). It's not designed to
  machine-apply diffs. Jane Street's ecosystem for this splits into:

  - patience_diff вҖ” the algorithm (what patdiff uses under the hood). Computes
  diffs but doesn't apply them.
  - patdiff вҖ” pretty printing + interactive modes on top.
  - No "patdiff-apply" in their stack вҖ” when they need to apply patches in their
   workflow, they go through git.

  So if you wanted patdiff's rendering quality and an applier, you'd still pair
  it with:
  - the patch library (what we have), or
  - git apply, or
  - a custom Myers-based applier.

  There's no free "same lib does both" win. The natural combos are:
  - Current: patch for diff+apply. Apply works; diff output is ugly.
  - patdiff + patch: pretty diff rendering + same apply. But adds Core/Async/re2
   deps.
  - git for diff + patch for apply: prettiest rendering, no new deps,
  dual-engine feels inconsistent.

  My vote still: git for rendering, patch for applying. Because:
  1. Rendering is user-facing вҖ” quality matters, git is what they read
  everywhere else.
  2. Applying is internal вҖ” patch library is fine for our small hunks, we don't
  gain much by changing it.

вқҜ but I am now worried of the quality of Patch apply as well                    

вҸә Reasonable worry. The patch library's apply has known fragile spots: trailing
  newline handling, context-line fuzz, CRLF/LF normalization. Our branch_diff.ml
   case (Write+Edit = exact bytes, yet walker still fired a human edit) is
  direct evidence of round-trip loss вҖ” the library reproduced almost the right
  content but not byte-exactly.

  Three honest paths:

  (a) Replace apply with git apply
  - Write stored patch to temp file, git apply on base, read output. Per-apply
  IPC cost but rock-solid.
  - Keeps the same storage schema.

  (b) Stop storing patches; store post-edit string state directly
  - For every edit, walker keeps the full resulting file content as a string.
  Materialise = lookup. Reconcile = string compare.
  - Zero algorithmic risk. No patch library at all.
  - More disk (store final strings per edit), but small for our sizes.
  - This is what I proposed earlier and you vetoed вҖ” but the veto was in a
  different context (you thought patch was fine). Given you're now worried about
   apply quality too, it's worth reconsidering.

  (c) Keep the patch library but add a correctness check
  - After each apply chain, compare walker's materialised string to what
  splicing produces directly. If they diverge вҶ’ warn, use the splice value.
  - Belt-and-suspenders; doesn't fix the root issue.

  My suggestion: (b). It's the least code, zero algorithmic risk, and matches
  the real shape of the problem (you don't actually need patches вҖ” you need
  "what does this file look like after Claude's N edits?"). The Patch library
  has never been the right tool for this particular job; it's a diff format
  parser, not a state tracker.

  Your call.

вҸә Walker rewritten on direct string state:

  - file_state = Content of string | Deleted | Absent вҖ” no more Patches of 
  Patch.t list, no Patch.diff/Patch.patch anywhere in the walker.
  - Write: just set_file file (Content new_string). Byte-exact.
  - Edit: find_substring + String.sub splice + concat вҶ’ set_file file (Content 
  new_content). Byte-exact.
  - Commit reconcile: string equality check. If mismatch вҶ’ human edit with
  before/actual from git, then snap state to Content actual.
  - Handler record slimmed to read_blob ~commit ~file вҖ” just pulls the blob,
  nothing more.

  Zero Patch library roundtrip risk. Test suite green.

  Now you can re-init from scratch and the branch_diff.ml case (and every
  similar one) should come out as just the Claude links, no phantom human. Human
   rows will only exist for genuine drift вҖ” content in commits that no Claude
  edit produces.

  rm -f .urme/db.sqlite* .urme/git-state.json
  dune exec bin/main.exe -- init -j 8

  prompts, no responses, no tool_uses. That part works.
  - BUT Claude CLI still writes a tiny ai-title metadata file (~100 bytes) per

вқҜ /export                                                                       
  вҺҝ  Export cancelled

вқҜ how would I export?                                                           

вҸә /export is a Claude Code built-in slash command. When you run it (type /export
   and enter), it should prompt you for:
  - A destination path (or auto-generate one)
  - Optionally a format (markdown/json)

  It dumps the current session's conversation transcript вҖ” user messages,
  assistant responses, tool uses вҖ” to that file.

  You did open it just now but cancelled (per the "Export cancelled" message).
  Try again and accept the prompt. If you want to script it, there's usually a
  non-interactive form too.

  Alternate ways:
  - Grab the JSONL while it's live: the active session is being written to
  ~/.claude/projects/-Users-corneliuhoffman-Documents-experience-agent/<session-
  id>.jsonl as you type. cp that file somewhere safe right now. Won't get the
  turns lost from cfdb7d82 but will at least preserve everything since.
  - claude history or similar: there may be a claude CLI subcommand to
  list/export past sessions вҖ” check claude --help | grep -i export.

  Recommend: run /export inside Claude Code and accept the prompt. Fastest way
  to lock in what you have.
